From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Species of flowering plant that produces cucumbers

For other uses, see [Cucumber (disambiguation)](/wiki/Cucumber_(disambiguation) "Cucumber (disambiguation)")
.

The **cucumber** (_**Cucumis sativus**_) is a widely-cultivated [creeping vine](/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants "Vine")
 plant in the family [Cucurbitaceae](/wiki/Cucurbitaceae "Cucurbitaceae")
 that bears cylindrical to spherical [fruits](/wiki/Fruit "Fruit")
, which are used as [culinary vegetables](/wiki/Culinary_vegetable "Culinary vegetable")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica-1)
 Considered an annual plant,[\[2\]](#cite_note-2)
 there are three main types of cucumber—slicing, [pickling](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
, and [seedless](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
—within which several [cultivars](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 have been created. The cucumber originates in [Asia](/wiki/Asia "Asia")
 extending from [India](/wiki/India "India")
, [Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal")
, [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh")
, [China](/wiki/China "China")
 ([Yunnan](/wiki/Yunnan "Yunnan")
, [Guizhou](/wiki/Guizhou "Guizhou")
, [Guangxi](/wiki/Guangxi "Guangxi")
), and [Northern Thailand](/wiki/Northern_Thailand "Northern Thailand")
,[\[3\]](#cite_note-3)
[\[4\]](#cite_note-4)
[\[5\]](#cite_note-powo-5)
[\[6\]](#cite_note-6)
 but now grows on most [continents](/wiki/Continent "Continent")
, and many different types of cucumber are grown commercially and traded on the [global market](/wiki/Global_market "Global market")
. In [North America](/wiki/North_America "North America")
, the term _[wild cucumber](/wiki/Wild_cucumber "Wild cucumber")
_ refers to plants in the [genera](/wiki/Genus "Genus")
 _[Echinocystis](/wiki/Echinocystis "Echinocystis")
_ and _[Marah](/wiki/Marah_(plant) "Marah (plant)")
_, though the two are not closely related.

Description
-----------

The cucumber is a [creeping vine](/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants "Vine")
 that roots in the ground and grows up [trellises](/wiki/Trellis_(agriculture) "Trellis (agriculture)")
 or other supporting frames, wrapping around supports with thin, spiraling [tendrils](/wiki/Tendrils "Tendrils")
.[\[7\]](#cite_note-7)
 The plant may also root in a [soilless medium](/wiki/Soilless_gardening "Soilless gardening")
, whereby it will sprawl along the ground in lieu of a supporting structure. The vine has large leaves that form a [canopy](/wiki/Canopy_(biology) "Canopy (biology)")
 over the fruits.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

The fruit of typical cultivars of cucumber is roughly [cylindrical](/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry) "Cylinder (geometry)")
, but elongated with tapered ends, and may be as large as 62 centimeters (24 in) long and 10 centimeters (4 in) in diameter.[\[8\]](#cite_note-ZhangLi2019-8)

Cucumber fruits consist of 95% water (see nutrition table). In [botanical](/wiki/Botany "Botany")
 terms, the cucumber is classified as a [_pepo_](/wiki/Pepo_(botany) "Pepo (botany)")
, a type of [botanical berry](/wiki/Berry_(botany) "Berry (botany)")
 with a hard outer rind and no internal divisions. However, much like [tomatoes](/wiki/Tomato "Tomato")
 and [squashes](/wiki/Cucurbita "Cucurbita")
, it is often perceived, prepared, and eaten as a [vegetable](/wiki/Vegetable "Vegetable")
.[\[9\]](#cite_note-9)

### Flowering and pollination

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG/150px-Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG)](/wiki/File:Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG)

_Cucumis sativus_ flower

|     |     |
| --- | --- |Genomic information
| [NCBI](/wiki/National_Center_for_Biotechnology_Information "National Center for Biotechnology Information")<br> genome ID | [1639](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/?term=1639) |
| [Ploidy](/wiki/Ploidy "Ploidy") | diploid |
| [Genome size](/wiki/Genome_size "Genome size") | 323.99 Mb |
| Sequenced [organelle](/wiki/Organelle "Organelle") | mitochondrion |
| Organelle size | 244.82 Mb |
| Year of completion | 2011 |

Most cucumber cultivars are seeded and require pollination. For this purpose, thousands of [honey](/wiki/Honey_bee "Honey bee")
 [beehives](/wiki/Beehive "Beehive")
 are annually carried to cucumber fields just before bloom. Cucumbers may also be pollinated via [bumblebees](/wiki/Bumblebee "Bumblebee")
 and several other bee species. Most cucumbers that require pollination are [self-incompatible](/wiki/Self_incompatibility_in_plants "Self incompatibility in plants")
, thus requiring the [pollen](/wiki/Pollen "Pollen")
 of another plant in order to form [seeds](/wiki/Seed "Seed")
 and fruit.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)
 Some self-compatible cultivars exist that are related to the 'Lemon cucumber' cultivar.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)

A few [cultivars](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 of cucumber are [parthenocarpic](/wiki/Parthenocarpy "Parthenocarpy")
, the [blossoms](/wiki/Blossom "Blossom")
 of which create [seedless fruit](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
 without [pollination](/wiki/Pollination "Pollination")
, which degrades the eating quality of these cultivar. In the [United States](/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_United_States "Agriculture in the United States")
, these are usually grown in [greenhouses](/wiki/Greenhouse "Greenhouse")
, where [bees](/wiki/Bee "Bee")
 are excluded. In [Europe](/wiki/Europe "Europe")
, they are grown outdoors in some regions, where bees are likewise excluded.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

Traditional cultivars produce male blossoms first, then female, in about equivalent numbers. Newer [gynoecious](/wiki/Gynoecious "Gynoecious")
 hybrid cultivars produce almost all female blossoms. They may have a [pollenizer](/wiki/Pollenizer "Pollenizer")
 cultivar interplanted, and the number of beehives per unit area is increased, but temperature changes induce male flowers even on these plants, which may be sufficient for pollination to occur.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)

In 2009, an international team of researchers announced they had sequenced the cucumber [genome](/wiki/Genome "Genome")
.[\[11\]](#cite_note-11)

A study of [genetic recombination](/wiki/Genetic_recombination "Genetic recombination")
 during [meiosis](/wiki/Meiosis "Meiosis")
 in cucumber provided a high resolution landscape of meiotic [DNA double strand-breaks](/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring) "DNA damage (naturally occurring)")
 and [genetic crossovers](/wiki/Chromosomal_crossover "Chromosomal crossover")
.[\[12\]](#cite_note-Wang2023-12)
 The average number of crossovers per chromosome per meiosis was 0.92 to 0.99.[\[12\]](#cite_note-Wang2023-12)

### Herbivore defense

[Phytochemicals](/wiki/Phytochemical "Phytochemical")
 in cucumbers may discourage natural [foraging](/wiki/Foraging "Foraging")
 by [herbivores](/wiki/Herbivore "Herbivore")
, such as insects, [nematodes](/wiki/Nematode "Nematode")
 or [wildlife](/wiki/Wildlife "Wildlife")
.[\[13\]](#cite_note-shang-13)
 As a possible defense mechanism, cucumbers produce [cucurbitacin C](/wiki/Cucurbitacin "Cucurbitacin")
,[\[14\]](#cite_note-:0a-14)
 which causes a [bitter taste](/wiki/Bitter_(taste) "Bitter (taste)")
 in some cucumber varieties. This potential mechanism is under preliminary research to identify whether cucumbers are able to deter herbivores and [environmental stresses](/wiki/Biotic_stress#Effect_on_plant_growth "Biotic stress")
 by using an intrinsic [chemical defense](/wiki/Chemical_defense#Plants "Chemical defense")
, particularly in the leaves, [cotyledons](/wiki/Cotyledon "Cotyledon")
, [pedicel](/wiki/Pedicel_(botany) "Pedicel (botany)")
, [carpopodium](/wiki/Caropodium "Caropodium")
, and fruit.[\[14\]](#cite_note-:0a-14)
[\[15\]](#cite_note-15)

| †Percentages estimated using [US recommendations](/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values "Reference Daily Intake")<br> for adults,[\[16\]](#cite_note-FDADailyValues-16)<br> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from [the National Academies](/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine "National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine")<br>.[\[17\]](#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-17) |     |

Raw cucumber (with [peel](/wiki/Peel_(fruit) "Peel (fruit)")
) is 95% water, 4% [carbohydrates](/wiki/Carbohydrate "Carbohydrate")
, 1% [protein](/wiki/Protein "Protein")
, and contains negligible [fat](/wiki/Fat "Fat")
. A 100-gram (3+1⁄2\-ounce) [reference serving](/wiki/Serving_size "Serving size")
 provides 65 kilojoules (16 kilocalories) of [food energy](/wiki/Food_energy "Food energy")
. It has a low content of [micronutrients](/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient")
: it is notable only for [vitamin K](/wiki/Vitamin_K "Vitamin K")
, at 14% of the [Daily Value](/wiki/Daily_Value "Daily Value")
 (table).

Depending on variety, cucumbers may have a mild [melon](/wiki/Melon "Melon")
 aroma and flavor, in part resulting from unsaturated [aldehydes](/wiki/Aldehyde "Aldehyde")
, such as [(E,Z)-nona-2,6-dienal](/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal "Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal")
, and the [_cis_\- and _trans_\-](/wiki/Cis%E2%80%93trans_isomerism "Cis–trans isomerism")
 [isomers](/wiki/Isomer "Isomer")
 of [2-nonenal](/wiki/2-Nonenal "2-Nonenal")
.[\[18\]](#cite_note-18)
 The slightly [bitter](/wiki/Bitter_(taste) "Bitter (taste)")
 taste of cucumber rind results from [cucurbitacins](/wiki/Cucurbitacins "Cucurbitacins")
.[\[19\]](#cite_note-19)

Varieties
---------

See also: [List of cucumber varieties](/wiki/List_of_cucumber_varieties "List of cucumber varieties")

In general [cultivation](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture")
, cucumbers are classified into three main [cultivar](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 groups: slicing, [pickled](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
, and [seedless/burpless](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
.

Uses
----

### Culinary

#### Slicing

Cucumbers grown to eat fresh are called _slicing cucumbers_. The main varieties of slicers mature on [vines](/wiki/Vine "Vine")
 with large leaves that provide shading.[\[20\]](#cite_note-20)

Slicers grown commercially for the North American market are generally longer, smoother, more uniform in color, and have much tougher skin. In contrast, those in other countries, often called [European cucumbers](/wiki/European_cucumber "European cucumber")
, are smaller and have thinner, more delicate skin, often with fewer seeds, thus are often sold in plastic skin for protection. This variety may also be called a _telegraph cucumber_, particularly in [Australasia](/wiki/Australasia "Australasia")
.[\[21\]](#cite_note-21)

#### Pickling

Main article: [Pickled cucumber](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")

[Pickling](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
 with [brine](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Brined_pickles "Pickled cucumber")
, sugar, [vinegar](/wiki/Vinegar "Vinegar")
, and spices creates various flavored products from cucumbers and other foods.[\[22\]](#cite_note-avi-22)
 Although any cucumber can be pickled, commercial pickles are made from cucumbers specially bred for uniformity of length-to-diameter ratio and lack of voids in the flesh. Those cucumbers intended for pickling, called _picklers_, grow to about 7 to 10 cm (3 to 4 in) long and 2.5 cm (1 in) wide. Compared to slicers, picklers tend to be shorter, thicker, less-regularly shaped, and have bumpy skin with tiny white or black-dotted spines. Color can vary from creamy yellow to pale or dark green.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

#### Gherkin

[Gherkins](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Gherkin "Pickled cucumber")
, also called _cornichons_,[\[23\]](#cite_note-kitchn-23)
 or _baby pickles_, are small cucumbers, typically those 2.5 to 12.5 centimetres (1 to 5 in) in length, often with bumpy skin, which are typically used for pickling.[\[24\]](#cite_note-zon-24)
[\[25\]](#cite_note-wifss-25)
[\[26\]](#cite_note-india-26)
 The word _gherkin_ comes from the early modern [Dutch](/wiki/Dutch_language "Dutch language")
 _gurken_ or _augurken_ ('small pickled cucumber').[\[27\]](#cite_note-27)
 The term is also used in the name for _[Cucumis anguria](/wiki/Cucumis_anguria "Cucumis anguria")
_, the _West Indian gherkin_, a closely related species.[\[28\]](#cite_note-28)

#### Burpless

Burpless cucumbers are sweeter and have a thinner skin than other varieties of cucumber. They are reputed to be easy to digest and to have a pleasant taste. They can grow as long as 60 centimeters (2 ft), are nearly seedless, and have a delicate skin. Most commonly grown in greenhouses, these [parthenocarpic](/wiki/Parthenocarpic "Parthenocarpic")
 cucumbers are often found in [grocery markets](/wiki/Grocery_store "Grocery store")
, [shrink-wrapped](/wiki/Shrink_wrap "Shrink wrap")
 in plastic. They are marketed as either burpless or seedless, as the seeds and skin of other varieties of cucumbers are said to give some people gas.[\[29\]](#cite_note-29)

Production
----------

In 2022, world production of cucumbers and gherkins was 95 million [tonnes](/wiki/Tonne "Tonne")
, led by China with 82% of the total.[\[30\]](#cite_note-faostat-30)

Cultivation history
-------------------

Cultivated for at least 3,000 years, the cultivated cucumbers _"Cucumis sativus"_ were domesticated in [India](/wiki/India "India")
 from wild "_C. sativus var. hardwickii_".[\[31\]](#cite_note-31)
[\[32\]](#cite_note-32)
[\[33\]](#cite_note-33)
 where a great many varieties have been observed, along with its closest living relative, _[Cucumis hystrix](/wiki/Cucumis_hystrix "Cucumis hystrix")
_.[\[34\]](#cite_note-34)
 Three main cultivar groups of cucumber are namely Eurasian cucumbers (slicing cucumbers eaten raw and immature), East Asian cucumbers (pickling cucumbers) and Xishuangbanna cucumbers. Based on demographic modelling, the East Asian C. sativus cultivars diverged from the Indian cultivars c. 2500 years ago.[\[35\]](#cite_note-35)
 It was probably introduced to Europe by the [Greeks](/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece "Agriculture in ancient Greece")
 or [Romans](/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Rome "Agriculture in ancient Rome")
. Records of cucumber cultivation appear in [France](/wiki/France "France")
 in the 9th century, [England](/wiki/Agriculture_in_England "Agriculture in England")
 in the 14th century, and in North America by the mid-16th century.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica-1)
[\[36\]](#cite_note-Renner_2007-36)
[\[37\]](#cite_note-Doijode-37)
[\[38\]](#cite_note-38)

### Roman Empire

According to [Pliny the Elder](/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder "Pliny the Elder")
, the Emperor [Tiberius](/wiki/Tiberius "Tiberius")
 had the cucumber on his table daily during summer and winter. In order to have it available for his table every day of the year, the Romans reportedly used artificial methods of growing (similar to the [greenhouse system](/wiki/Greenhouse "Greenhouse")
), whereby _mirrorstone_ refers to Pliny's _lapis specularis_, believed to have been sheet [mica](/wiki/Mica "Mica")
:[\[39\]](#cite_note-AncientInventions-39)
[\[40\]](#cite_note-40)

> Indeed, he was never without it; for he had raised beds made in frames upon wheels, by means of which the cucumbers were moved and exposed to the full heat of the sun; while, in winter, they were withdrawn, and placed under the protection of frames glazed with mirrorstone.
> 
> — Pliny the Elder, _[Natural History](/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny) "Natural History (Pliny)")
> _ XIX.xxiii, "Vegetables of a Cartilaginous Nature—Cucumbers. Pepones"

Reportedly, they were also cultivated in _specularia_, cucumber houses glazed with oiled cloth.[\[39\]](#cite_note-AncientInventions-39)
 Pliny describes the Italian fruit as very small, probably like a [gherkin](/wiki/Gherkin "Gherkin")
. He also describes the preparation of a medication known as _elaterium_. However, some scholars\[_[who?](/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch")\
_\] believe that he was instead referring to _[Ecballium elaterium](/wiki/Ecballium_elaterium "Ecballium elaterium")
_, known in pre-[Linnean](/wiki/Linnean_nomenclature "Linnean nomenclature")
 times as _Cucumis silvestris_ or _Cucumis asininus_ ('wild cucumber' or 'donkey cucumber'), a species different from the common cucumber.[\[41\]](#cite_note-41)
 Pliny also writes about several other varieties of cucumber, including the cultivated cucumber,[\[42\]](#cite_note-42)
 and remedies from the different types (9 from the cultivated; 5 from the "anguine;" and 26 from the "wild").

### Middle Ages

[Charlemagne](/wiki/Charlemagne "Charlemagne")
 had cucumbers grown in his gardens in the 8th/9th century. They were reportedly introduced into England in the early 14th century, lost, then reintroduced approximately 250 years later. The [Spaniards](/wiki/Spaniards "Spaniards")
 (through the [Italian](/wiki/Italian_people "Italian people")
 [Christopher Columbus](/wiki/Christopher_Columbus "Christopher Columbus")
) brought cucumbers to [Haiti](/wiki/Haiti "Haiti")
 in 1494. In 1535, [Jacques Cartier](/wiki/Jacques_Cartier "Jacques Cartier")
, a French explorer, found "very great cucumbers" grown on the site of what is now [Montreal](/wiki/Montreal "Montreal")
.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

### Early-modern age

[![trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal, or cucumber aldehyde](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png/250px-Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png)](/wiki/File:Trans-2,cis-6-Nonadienal.png)

[Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal](/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal "Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal")
, or _cucumber aldehyde_, is a component of the distinctive aroma of cucumbers.

Throughout the 16th century, European trappers, traders, [bison](/wiki/Bison "Bison")
 hunters, and explorers bartered for the products of American Indian [agriculture](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture")
. The tribes of the [Great Plains](/wiki/Great_Plains "Great Plains")
 and the [Rocky Mountains](/wiki/Rocky_Mountains "Rocky Mountains")
 learned from the Spanish how to grow European crops. The farmers on the Great Plains included the [Mandan](/wiki/Mandan "Mandan")
 and [Abenaki](/wiki/Abenaki "Abenaki")
. They obtained cucumbers and [watermelons](/wiki/Watermelon "Watermelon")
 from the Spanish, and added them to the crops they were already growing, including several varieties of [corn](/wiki/Maize "Maize")
 and [beans](/wiki/Bean "Bean")
, [pumpkins](/wiki/Pumpkin "Pumpkin")
, [squash](/wiki/Squash_(fruit) "Squash (fruit)")
, and [gourd](/wiki/Gourd "Gourd")
 plants.[\[43\]](#cite_note-43)
 The [Iroquois](/wiki/Iroquois "Iroquois")
 were also growing them when the first Europeans visited them.[\[44\]](#cite_note-44)

In 1630, the Reverend [Francis Higginson](/wiki/Francis_Higginson "Francis Higginson")
 produced a book called _New-Englands Plantation_ in which, describing a garden on Conant's Island in [Boston Harbor](/wiki/Boston_Harbor "Boston Harbor")
 known as _The Governor's Garden_, he states:[\[45\]](#cite_note-45)

> The countrie aboundeth naturally with store of roots of great varietie \[_[sic](/wiki/Sic "Sic")\
> _\] and good to eat. Our turnips, parsnips, and carrots are here both bigger and sweeter than is ordinary to be found in England. Here are store of pompions, cowcumbers, and other things of that nature which I know not...

In _New England Prospect_ (1633, England), William Wood published observations he made in 1629 in America:[\[46\]](#cite_note-46)

> The ground affords very good kitchin gardens, for Turneps, Parsnips, Carrots, Radishes, and Pompions, Muskmillons, Isquoter-squashes, coucumbars, Onyons, and whatever grows well in England grows as well there, many things being better and larger. \[_[sic](/wiki/Sic "Sic")\
> _\]

### Age of Enlightenment and later

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg/220px-Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg)](/wiki/File:Lobster,_Crab,_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg)

_Lobster, Crab, and a Cucumber_ by [William Henry Hunt](/wiki/William_Henry_Hunt_(painter) "William Henry Hunt (painter)")
 (watercolour, 1826 or 1827)

In the later 17th century, a prejudice developed against uncooked vegetables and fruits. A number of articles in contemporary health publications stated that uncooked plants brought on summer diseases and should be forbidden to children. The cucumber kept this reputation for an inordinate period of time, "fit only for consumption by cows," which some believe is why it gained the name, _cowcumber_.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

[Samuel Pepys](/wiki/Samuel_Pepys "Samuel Pepys")
 wrote in his diary on 22 August 1663:[\[47\]](#cite_note-47)

> \[T\]his day Sir W. Batten tells me that Mr. Newburne is dead of eating cowcumbers, of which the other day I heard of another, I think.

John Evelyn in 1699 wrote that the cucumber, 'however dress'd, was thought fit to be thrown away, being accounted little better than poyson (poison)'.[\[48\]](#cite_note-48)
[\[49\]](#cite_note-49)

According to 18th-century British writer [Samuel Johnson](/wiki/Samuel_Johnson "Samuel Johnson")
, it was commonly said among English physicians that a cucumber "should be well sliced, and dressed with pepper and vinegar, and then thrown out, as good for nothing."[\[50\]](#cite_note-50)

A copper [etching](/wiki/Etching "Etching")
 made by Maddalena Bouchard between 1772 and 1793 shows this plant to have smaller, almost bean-shaped fruits, and small yellow flowers. The small form of the cucumber is figured in [Herbals](/wiki/Herbal "Herbal")
 of the 16th century, however stating that "\[i\]f hung in a tube while in blossom, the Cucumber will grow to a most surprising length."\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png)

Look up _**[cucumber](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cucumber "wiktionary:cucumber")
**_, _**[Cucumis sativus](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Cucumis_sativus "wiktionary:Cucumis sativus")
**_, or _**[cuke](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cuke "wiktionary:cuke")
**_ in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.

A **[cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
** is an edible vegetable.

**Cucumber** may also refer to:

Arts and entertainment
----------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Arts and entertainment")\
\]

*   [_Cucumber_ (British TV series)](/wiki/Cucumber_(British_TV_series) "Cucumber (British TV series)")
    , a 2015 British television series
*   [Cucumber (card game)](/wiki/Cucumber_(card_game) "Cucumber (card game)")
    , a north European card game of Swedish origin
*   [_Cucumber_ (Canadian TV series)](/wiki/Cucumber_(Canadian_TV_series) "Cucumber (Canadian TV series)")
    , a Canadian children's television series originally broadcast in the 1970s
*   [The Cucumbers](/wiki/The_Cucumbers "The Cucumbers")
    , a 1980s power pop/new wave band from New Jersey

Plants
------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Plants")\
\]

*   [Armenian cucumber](/wiki/Armenian_cucumber "Armenian cucumber")
    , an edible vegetable closely related to the common cucumber
*   [Cucumber tree](/wiki/Cucumber_tree "Cucumber tree")
    , several unrelated trees
*   [Wild cucumber (disambiguation)](/wiki/Wild_cucumber_(disambiguation) "Wild cucumber (disambiguation)")
    , several species of plants similar to cucumbers

Other
-----

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Other")\
\]

*   [Cucumber (software)](/wiki/Cucumber_(software) "Cucumber (software)")
    , a behavior-driven development tool
*   [Cucumber, West Virginia](/wiki/Cucumber,_West_Virginia "Cucumber, West Virginia")
    , a community in West Virginia
*   [Cucumber beetle](/wiki/Cucumber_beetle "Cucumber beetle")
    , a type of beetle
*   [Sea cucumber](/wiki/Sea_cucumber "Sea cucumber")
     (_Holothuroidea_), a type of animal
*   "[Cucumber King](/wiki/Cucumber_King "Cucumber King")
    " (Nyaung-u Sawrahan), a ruler of the kingdom of Pagan in what is now Burma
*   The top level on the 1 to 4 EHS scale developed by the [European Association of Urology](/wiki/European_Association_of_Urology "European Association of Urology")

The [Sanskrit](/wiki/Sanskrit "Sanskrit")
 term **adhiṣṭhāna** ([Sanskrit](/wiki/Sanskrit_language "Sanskrit language")
: अधिष्ठान; [Tibetan](/wiki/Tibetan_script "Tibetan script")
: བྱིན་རླབས, [THL](/wiki/THL_Simplified_Phonetic_Transcription "THL Simplified Phonetic Transcription")
: _jinlap_; [Japanese](/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language")
: 加持 _kaji_; [Thai](/wiki/Thai_language "Thai language")
: [อธิษฐาน](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E0%B8%AD%E0%B8%98%E0%B8%B4%E0%B8%A9%E0%B8%90%E0%B8%B2%E0%B8%99 "wikt:อธิษฐาน")
 _àtíttǎan_) is the name for blessings or inspiration that a Buddhist may receive from a [Buddha](/wiki/Buddhahood "Buddhahood")
, [bodhisattva](/wiki/Bodhisattva "Bodhisattva")
 or [guru](/wiki/Guru "Guru")
. The Sanskrit term has various meanings in [Mahayana](/wiki/Mahayana "Mahayana")
 and [Vajrayana](/wiki/Vajrayana "Vajrayana")
 Buddhism, and can also mean [the raised base on which a temple stands](/wiki/Jagati_(temple) "Jagati (temple)")
.

Nomenclature, orthography and etymology
---------------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Nomenclature, orthography and etymology")\
\]

_Adhiṣṭhāna_(_m_) is a term with multiple meanings: seat; basis; substratum; ground; support; and abode.[\[1\]](#cite_note-1)
 The Monier-Williams Sanskrit-English Dictionary Online holds the following semantic field for _adhiṣṭhāna_:

1.  \[noun\] standing by, being at hand, approach
2.  standing or resting upon
3.  a basis, base
4.  the standing-place of the warrior upon the car
5.  a position, site, residence, abode, seat
6.  a settlement, town, standing over
7.  government, authority, power
8.  a precedent, rule
9.  a benediction (Buddhism)[\[2\]](#cite_note-2)
    

[Francesca Fremantle](/wiki/Francesca_Fremantle "Francesca Fremantle")
 gives an etymology of Sanskrit _adhiṣṭhāna_ and Tibetan _jinlap_: "The Sanskrit word literally means "standing over" and conveys ideas of taking possession, dwelling within, presence, protection, and sovereignty. The Tibetan literally means "an engulfing wave or flood of splendor and power."[\[3\]](#cite_note-3)

Dan Martin opines that the Chinese term for adhiṣṭhāna influenced the Tibetan:

> _Byin-rlabs_ is commonly glossed as 'gift wave', but it more properly goes back to a literal translation of a Chinese word which was almost certainly made during the earliest introduction of Buddhism into Tibet in the seventh or eighth centuries. It is not a literal translation of the Sanskrit Buddhists term adhisthana. Its actual, or rather its philologically correct, meaning is 'received by (way of) giving'.[\[4\]](#cite_note-4)

Vajrayana
---------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Vajrayana")\
\]

### Tibetan Buddhism

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Tibetan Buddhism")\
\]

[Tsultrim Allione](/wiki/Tsultrim_Allione "Tsultrim Allione")
 points out that in [Tibetan Buddhism](/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism "Tibetan Buddhism")
 adhistana blessings are an important part of the [pointing-out instruction](/wiki/Pointing-out_instruction "Pointing-out instruction")
 received from the [guru](/wiki/Guru "Guru")
 and [lineage](/wiki/Lineage_(Buddhism) "Lineage (Buddhism)")
.[\[5\]](#cite_note-5)
 Receiving these blessings is dependent on the student having proper motivation, aspiration and intentionality ([bodhicitta](/wiki/Bodhicitta "Bodhicitta")
) and sufficient "devotion" (Sanskrit: [bhakti](/wiki/Bhakti "Bhakti")
). These blessings may be received from the student's [guru](/wiki/Guru "Guru")
 during [initiation](/wiki/Initiation "Initiation")
, from the [yidam](/wiki/Yidam "Yidam")
 during [deity yoga](/wiki/Vajrayana#Deity_yoga "Vajrayana")
, or simply from being in the presence of holy objects such as a [stupa](/wiki/Stupa "Stupa")
 or the [śarīra](/wiki/%C5%9Aar%C4%ABra "Śarīra")
, "relics", kept inside them. These objects are held to emanate or incite _adhiṣṭhāna_ "blessings, grace" within the [mindstream](/wiki/Mindstream "Mindstream")
 and experience of those connected to them.[\[6\]](#cite_note-6)

#### Stream of blessings

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Stream of blessings")\
\]

In the Indo-Himalayan lineages of Vajrayana, where traditions of [Tantra](/wiki/Tantra "Tantra")
 were introduced in the first wave of translations of Sanskrit texts into [Old Tibetan](/wiki/Old_Tibetan "Old Tibetan")
 from the 8th century onwards, the term chosen by the community of [lotsawa](/wiki/Lotsawa "Lotsawa")
 "translators", which importantly is one of the most concerted translation efforts in documented history, chose to render adhiṣṭhāna as [Tibetan](/wiki/Tibetan_script "Tibetan script")
: བྱིན་རླབས, [THL](/wiki/THL_Simplified_Phonetic_Transcription "THL Simplified Phonetic Transcription")
: _jinlap_. This metaphorical usage of "stream, wave, thread, continuum" is reinforced in philosophy with the [mindstream](/wiki/Mindstream "Mindstream")
 doctrine and its relationship to tantric [sādhanā](/wiki/S%C4%81dhan%C4%81 "Sādhanā")
, where it is used in visualizations and invocations, particularly in relation to the [Three Vajras](/wiki/Three_Vajras "Three Vajras")
 of [Padmasambhava](/wiki/Padmasambhava "Padmasambhava")
 and depicted in Indo-Tibetan Buddhist and [Bon](/wiki/Bon "Bon")
 iconography such as representations of the [Adi-Buddha](/wiki/Adi-Buddha "Adi-Buddha")
 and [Tapihritsa](/wiki/Tapihritsa "Tapihritsa")
. Martin Mills, in a modern political and power-relations dissection of _jinlap_ in relation to hierarchical structures of the [Gelug](/wiki/Gelug "Gelug")
, a [Sarma](/wiki/Sarma_(Tibetan_Buddhism) "Sarma (Tibetan Buddhism)")
 (second-wave) school, holds that:

> The acceptance of offerings by worldly deities and spirits was felt very strongly to oblige the recipient to act in favour of the donor, and particularly to act as their protector (strungma), a term widely used by householders to describe the various numina that inhabited their houses. This protection was seen as being a blessing (chinlabs) which descended upon the offerer from above in the manner of a stream. This metaphor of the stream and its pure source is an important one, and is a central idiom by which hierarchical relations, either in hospitality gatherings, offering practices, or religious teachings, were conceived and spoken about, emphasising once again the salience of height as designating relations with social superiors and preceptors.[\[7\]](#cite_note-7)

An example of this sādhanā is described in _The Prayer of Inspiration_ known as "_The Falling Rain of Blessings_" (gsol 'debs byin rlabs char 'bebs) (from the Yang Zab Nyingpo).[\[8\]](#cite_note-8)

### Shingon Buddhism

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Shingon Buddhism")\
\]

In [Shingon Buddhism](/wiki/Shingon_Buddhism "Shingon Buddhism")
, an extant non-Himalayan school of Vajrayana, practices involving mantras, mudras, and visualisation exercises aim at achieving _[honzon](/wiki/Honzon "Honzon")
 kaji_ or union with the deity. According to Shingon priest Eijun Eidson:

> Honzon simply refers to the main deity in any given ritual. Kaji refers to the enhancement of a sentient being's power through the Buddha's power (Nyorai-kaji-riki), and it translates the Sanskrit word adhisthana.[\[9\]](#cite_note-9)

Minoru Kiyota identifies three kinds of adhiṣṭhāna in the theory and practice of [Shingon Buddhism](/wiki/Shingon_Buddhism "Shingon Buddhism")
:

1.  [mudra](/wiki/Mudra "Mudra")
    , the finger sign
2.  [dhāraṇī](/wiki/Dh%C4%81ra%E1%B9%87%C4%AB "Dhāraṇī")
    , secret verses
3.  [yoga](/wiki/Yoga "Yoga")
    , through [meditation practices](/wiki/Tantra_techniques_(Vajrayana) "Tantra techniques (Vajrayana)")
    .[\[10\]](#cite_note-10)
    

The term _adhiṣṭhāna_ is also used to describe the transformative power of the [Buddha](/wiki/Buddhahood "Buddhahood")
. According to [D. T. Suzuki](/wiki/D._T._Suzuki "D. T. Suzuki")
:

> The Buddha is creative life itself, he creates himself in innumerable forms with all the means native to him. This is called his adhisthana, as it were, emanating from his personality. The idea of _adhiṣṭhāna_ is one of the Mahayana landmarks in the history of Indian Buddhism and it is at the same time the beginning of the 'other-power' (tariki in Japanese) school as distinguished from the 'self-power' (jiriki).[\[11\]](#cite_note-11)

#### Cucumber blessing

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Adhi%E1%B9%A3%E1%B9%ADh%C4%81na&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: Cucumber blessing")\
\]

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Cucumber_blessing_in_Kailua-Kona.jpg/200px-Cucumber_blessing_in_Kailua-Kona.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber_blessing_in_Kailua-Kona.jpg)

Cucumbers being blessed at a Shingon Buddhist temple in [Kailua, Hawaii County, Hawaii](/wiki/Kailua,_Hawaii_County,_Hawaii "Kailua, Hawaii County, Hawaii")
.

The cucumber blessing ([Japanese](/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language")
: きゅうり加持) is an _adhiṣṭhāna_ practised at Shingon Buddhist temples in [summer](/wiki/Summer "Summer")
. In a cucumber blessing meeting, the [priest](/wiki/Priest "Priest")
 and believers together pray that they can pass the season of hot summer in [good health](/wiki/Health "Health")
 like fresh [cucumbers](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
.[\[12\]](#cite_note-12)
 [Kūkai](/wiki/K%C5%ABkai "Kūkai")
, the founder of Shingon Buddhism, is said to have initiated this practice of blessing.

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Indian dessert

**Cucumber cake** is a [cake](/wiki/Cake "Cake")
 prepared using [cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
 as a primary ingredient. Additional ingredients may include typical cake ingredients such as flour, sugar, eggs and leavening.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Shenoy_2016-1)
 It may be served as a dessert cake,[\[2\]](#cite_note-mid-day_2016-2)
 and some versions are topped with an [icing](/wiki/Icing_(food) "Icing (food)")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Shenoy_2016-1)

Tavsoli\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_cake&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Tavsoli")\
\]
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Cucumber cake may be referred to as _tavsoli_ or _tausali_, and is a dish in [Goan cuisine](/wiki/Goan_cuisine "Goan cuisine")
.[\[3\]](#cite_note-Awchat_2009-3)
[\[4\]](#cite_note-Monteiro_2015-4)
 _Tavsoli_ is prepared using cucumber, and additional ingredients may include rice, coconut, [jaggery](/wiki/Jaggery "Jaggery")
, [ghee](/wiki/Ghee "Ghee")
, cashews and [cardamom](/wiki/Cardamom "Cardamom")
.[\[3\]](#cite_note-Awchat_2009-3)
[\[4\]](#cite_note-Monteiro_2015-4)
 [Turmeric](/wiki/Turmeric "Turmeric")
 leaves may be placed atop the cake prior to baking, which adds flavor.[\[3\]](#cite_note-Awchat_2009-3)
 It may be served as a breakfast dish,[\[5\]](#cite_note-Aayis_2006-5)
 and may be prepared in the style of a [dhokla](/wiki/Dhokla "Dhokla")
[\[6\]](#cite_note-Food_and_Health-6)
 and [steamed](/wiki/Steaming "Steaming")
.[\[7\]](#cite_note-Amit_2016-7)

See also\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_cake&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: See also")\
\]
----------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------------

*   [![icon](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Foodlogo2.svg/32px-Foodlogo2.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Foodlogo2.svg)
    [Food portal](/wiki/Portal:Food "Portal:Food")

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Juice derived from cucumbers

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Cucumber_juice.jpg/260px-Cucumber_juice.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber_juice.jpg)

Cucumber juice

**Cucumber juice** is the [juice](/wiki/Juice "Juice")
 derived from [cucumbers](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
 produced by squeezing or pressing it.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Balch_2003_p._36-1)
 Cucumbers are 98% water.[\[2\]](#cite_note-Publishing_2013_p._58-2)

Cucumber juice is used in beverages such as [cocktails](/wiki/Cocktail "Cocktail")
 like the [Bloody Mary](/wiki/Bloody_Mary_(cocktail) "Bloody Mary (cocktail)")
,[\[3\]](#cite_note-OHara_Nash_1999_p._92-3)
 dishes such as [cucumber soup](/wiki/Cucumber_soup "Cucumber soup")
,[\[4\]](#cite_note-Sharma_2005_p._179-4)
 and in dips and salad dressings, such as [green goddess dressing](/wiki/Green_goddess_dressing "Green goddess dressing")
.[\[5\]](#cite_note-Hesser_2010_p._142-5)
 Cucumber juice has significant amounts of [potassium](/wiki/Potassium "Potassium")
[\[1\]](#cite_note-Balch_2003_p._36-1)
 and is high in vitamin A.[\[6\]](#cite_note-Watson_2014_p._104-6)
 It also contains [sterol](/wiki/Sterol "Sterol")
[\[7\]](#cite_note-Pocket_Books_2002_p._119-7)
 and significant amounts of [silicon](/wiki/Silicon "Silicon")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Balch_2003_p._36-1)

|     |     |
| --- | --- |Cucumber juice
| Nutritional value per 1 ounce |     |
| [Energy](/wiki/Food_energy "Food energy") | 9 kcal (38 kJ) |
|     |     |
| **[Carbohydrates](/wiki/Carbohydrate "Carbohydrate")<br>** | 1 g |
|     |     |
|     |     |
| **[Vitamins](/wiki/Vitamin "Vitamin")<br>** | **Quantity**<br><br>**%DV**† |
| [Vitamin C](/wiki/Vitamin_C "Vitamin C") | 2%<br><br>2 mg |
|     |     |
|     |     |
| **[Minerals](/wiki/Mineral_(nutrient) "Mineral (nutrient)")<br>** | **Quantity**<br><br>**%DV**† |
| [Calcium](/wiki/Calcium_in_biology#Humans "Calcium in biology") | 3%<br><br>40 mg |
| [Iron](/wiki/Human_iron_metabolism "Human iron metabolism") | 1%<br><br>.1 mg |
| [Potassium](/wiki/Potassium_in_biology "Potassium in biology") | 1%<br><br>41 mg |
| [Sodium](/wiki/Sodium_in_biology "Sodium in biology") | 0%<br><br>2 mg |
|     |     |
| * * *<br><br>Nutritional content source:[\[8\]](#cite_note-MFP-8) |     |
| †Percentages estimated using [US recommendations](/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values "Reference Daily Intake")<br> for adults,[\[9\]](#cite_note-FDADailyValues-9)<br> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from [the National Academies](/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine "National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine")<br>.[\[10\]](#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-10) |     |

Cucumber juice is used as an ingredient in cosmetics, soaps, shampoos, and lotions,[\[11\]](#cite_note-Katsambas_Lotti_2003_p._473-11)
[\[12\]](#cite_note-Digest_2015_p._646-12)
 and in [eau de toilette](/wiki/Eau_de_toilette "Eau de toilette")
 and perfumes.[\[13\]](#cite_note-National_Association_of_Retail_Druggists._1911_p._649-13)

It was used in Russian [traditional medicine](/wiki/Traditional_medicine "Traditional medicine")
 to aid in the treatment of respiratory tract inflammation and to reduce lingering cough.[\[14\]](#cite_note-Zevin_Altman_Zevin_1997_p._65-14)
 In other traditions it was used to soothe [heartburn](/wiki/Heartburn "Heartburn")
 and reduce acid in the stomach.[\[15\]](#cite_note-Shealy_2012_p._75-15)
 For skin, it has been used to soothe burns and rashes.[\[15\]](#cite_note-Shealy_2012_p._75-15)
[\[14\]](#cite_note-Zevin_Altman_Zevin_1997_p._65-14)
[\[16\]](#cite_note-Duke_1993_p._203-16)
 Cucumber juice has been described as a repellent against [wood lice](/wiki/Woodlouse "Woodlouse")
 and [fish-moths](/wiki/Silverfish "Silverfish")
.[\[16\]](#cite_note-Duke_1993_p._203-16)

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Indian condiment

|     |     |
| --- | --- |Cucumber Raita
| [![Cucumber raita served with the garnishing of fresh coriander leaves.](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Cucumber-raita.jpg/220px-Cucumber-raita.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber-raita.jpg "Cucumber raita served with the garnishing of fresh coriander leaves.")<br><br>Cucumber raita served with the garnishing of fresh coriander leaves. |     |
| Place of origin | [India](/wiki/India "India") |
| Region or state | [Indian Subcontinent](/wiki/Indian_Subcontinent "Indian Subcontinent") |
| Main ingredients | [Cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")<br>, [dahi](/wiki/Dahi_(curd) "Dahi (curd)")<br> (yogurt), [Green Chilli](/w/index.php?title=Green_Chilli&action=edit&redlink=1 "Green Chilli (page does not exist)") |
| *    [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg)<br>     [Media: Cucumber Raita](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Cucumber_raita "commons:Category:Cucumber raita") |     |

**Cucumber Raita** is a variation of [raita](/wiki/Raita "Raita")
 that can be used as a [dip](/wiki/Dip_(food) "Dip (food)")
 or a [salad](/wiki/Salad "Salad")
. It is made by mixing together freshly chopped [cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
, [green chillies](/wiki/Green_chillies "Green chillies")
, and [dahi](/wiki/Dahi_(curd) "Dahi (curd)")
 (yogurt), and optionally finely chopped [tomato](/wiki/Tomato "Tomato")
 and [onion](/wiki/Onion "Onion")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Sedgwick-1)
[\[2\]](#cite_note-Orient-2)

It is especially popular in the summer months as it helps to beat the heat.[\[3\]](#cite_note-3)
 It is often served as a side accompaniment with the main course in [Indian cuisine](/wiki/Indian_cuisine "Indian cuisine")
.

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Thin slices of cucumber between two slices of crustless white bread

|     |     |
| --- | --- |
| [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg) | This article **needs additional citations for [verification](/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")<br>**. | [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Cucumber_sandwiches_%288768307255%29.jpg/250px-Cucumber_sandwiches_%288768307255%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber_sandwiches_(8768307255).jpg)<br><br>Cucumber sandwiches stacked on a plate |     |
| Type | [Tea sandwich](/wiki/Tea_sandwich "Tea sandwich") |
| Course | Afternoon tea |
| Place of origin | [United Kingdom](/wiki/United_Kingdom "United Kingdom") |
| Region or state | United Kingdom and United States |
| Main ingredients | [White bread](/wiki/White_bread "White bread")<br>, [cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")<br>, [butter](/wiki/Butter "Butter") |
| Variations | [Brown bread](/wiki/Brown_bread "Brown bread") |
| *    [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg)<br>     [Media: Cucumber sandwiches](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Cucumber_sandwiches "commons:Category:Cucumber sandwiches") |     |

The traditional **cucumber sandwich** is a crustless [tea sandwich](/wiki/Tea_sandwich "Tea sandwich")
 (or finger sandwich) composed of thin slices of [cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
 situated between two thin slices of lightly buttered [white bread](/wiki/White_bread "White bread")
. The sandwich originated in the United Kingdom, and modern variants, largely of United States origin, introduce [cream cheese](/wiki/Cream_cheese "Cream cheese")
, [mayonnaise](/wiki/Mayonnaise "Mayonnaise")
, chopped [dill](/wiki/Dill "Dill")
 or spices, and [salmon](/wiki/Salmon "Salmon")
, and may substitute [brown bread](/wiki/Brown_bread "Brown bread")
. One specific US variant includes [benedictine](/wiki/Benedictine_(spread) "Benedictine (spread)")
, a green [soft spread](/wiki/Soft_spread "Soft spread")
 made from cucumbers and [cream cheese](/wiki/Cream_cheese "Cream cheese")
.

Cucumber sandwiches are most often served for a light snack or for afternoon tea, a formal light meal served in the late afternoon, or in the early evening before the main [supper](/wiki/Supper "Supper")
. Cucumber sandwiches are also traditionally served in the [tea break](/wiki/Tea_break "Tea break")
 at club [cricket](/wiki/Cricket "Cricket")
 matches in England.

Cultural and historical associations
------------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_sandwich&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Cultural and historical associations")\
\]

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Flickr_bitboy_204619671--Cucumber_sandwiches_with_tea.jpg/220px-Flickr_bitboy_204619671--Cucumber_sandwiches_with_tea.jpg)](/wiki/File:Flickr_bitboy_204619671--Cucumber_sandwiches_with_tea.jpg)

Cucumber sandwiches and tea, as served at [Kensington Palace](/wiki/Kensington_Palace "Kensington Palace")

Cucumber sandwiches formed an integral part of the stereotypical afternoon tea affair. By contrast, people of the era's lower working classes were thought to prefer a coarser but more satisfying protein-filled sandwich, in a "meat tea" that might substitute for [supper](/wiki/Supper "Supper")
.

Some writers have attempted to draw out an association between the daintiness of the sandwich and the perceived effeteness of the UK's [aristocracy](/wiki/Aristocracy "Aristocracy")
. Cucumber sandwiches are often used as a kind of shorthand in [novels](/wiki/Novel "Novel")
 and [films](/wiki/Film "Film")
 to identify upper-class people, occasionally in a derogatory manner. In the first act of [Oscar Wilde](/wiki/Oscar_Wilde "Oscar Wilde")
's _[The Importance of Being Earnest](/wiki/The_Importance_of_Being_Earnest "The Importance of Being Earnest")
_ (1895), cucumber sandwiches that have expressly been ordered and prepared for [Lady Bracknell](/wiki/Lady_Bracknell "Lady Bracknell")
's expected visit are all voraciously eaten beforehand by her nephew and host, Algernon Moncrieff; consequently he is forced to tell a little lie, with his butler's connivance: namely that "there were no cucumbers in the market this morning... not even for ready money". In addition, the sandwiches were once considered appropriate delicacies to offer to visiting clergy, in times when such visits were still a common feature of English middle class life.

The popularity of the cucumber sandwich as an upper-class "dainty" reached its zenith in the [Edwardian era](/wiki/Edwardian_era "Edwardian era")
, when cheap labour and plentiful [coal](/wiki/Coal "Coal")
 enabled new techniques of producing cucumbers in [hotbeds](/wiki/Hotbed_(gardening) "Hotbed (gardening)")
 under glass through most of the year. With the declining popularity of tea as a meal in the United Kingdom, there was a corresponding decline in the popularity of cucumber sandwiches, but they are still frequently served at teas, luncheons, and gatherings. Most English cricket clubs supply malt vinegar and ground pepper to dash inside the sandwich, and this is the simplest form commonly used in England.

Cucumber sandwiches are often eaten in the summer months or in warmer climates, such as in parts of [India](/wiki/India "India")
. The English influence on Indian culture has made the sandwiches popular during cricket matches and weekend [picnics](/wiki/Picnic "Picnic")
. The Indian variant is flavoured with green [chutney](/wiki/Chutney "Chutney")
 and sometimes contains slices of boiled potatoes. [Indian Airlines](/wiki/Indian_Airlines "Indian Airlines")
 served cucumber sandwiches as part of its usual vegetarian inflight meal in short-haul domestic flights, until its merger in 2011.

Preparation
-----------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_sandwich&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Preparation")\
\]

|     |     |
| --- | --- |
| [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg) | This section **does not [cite](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources "Wikipedia:Citing sources")<br> any [sources](/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability "Wikipedia:Verifiability")<br>**. Please help [improve this section](/wiki/Special:EditPage/Cucumber_sandwich "Special:EditPage/Cucumber sandwich")<br> by [adding citations to reliable sources](/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners "Help:Referencing for beginners")<br>. Unsourced material may be challenged and [removed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence "Wikipedia:Verifiability")<br>. _(November 2020)_ _([Learn how and when to remove this message](/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal "Help:Maintenance template removal")<br>)_ |

A wide-bladed knife is used to obtain thin slices from a [Pullman loaf](/wiki/Pullman_loaf "Pullman loaf")
. According to one measure,\[_[which?](/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words "Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words")\
_\] daylight should pass through the resulting fine [pores](/wiki/Pores_(bread) "Pores (bread)")
 of the bread. The slices of bread are buttered all the way to the edges in the thinnest coating, which is only to prevent the bread from becoming damp with cucumber juice, and the slices of peeled cucumber, which have been dashed with [salt](/wiki/Salt "Salt")
 and [lemon](/wiki/Lemon "Lemon")
 juice, are placed in the sandwich just before serving to prevent the sandwich from becoming damp. The crusts of the bread are cut away cleanly, creating [tea sandwiches](/wiki/Tea_sandwich "Tea sandwich")
.

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Type of soft drink

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Mr._Q_Cumber_Cucumber_Soda_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Mr._Q_Cumber_Cucumber_Soda_%28cropped%29.JPG)](/wiki/File:Mr._Q_Cumber_Cucumber_Soda_(cropped).JPG)

Mr. Q Cucumber Soda

**Cucumber soda** is a type of [soda](/wiki/Soft_drink "Soft drink")
 made by various manufacturers including Mr. Q Cumber. [Pepsi](/wiki/Pepsi "Pepsi")
 offers an ice [cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
 flavor in some markets.[\[1\]](#cite_note-1)
 It is also made by home [soda makers](/wiki/Soda_maker "Soda maker")
.[\[2\]](#cite_note-2)

Appearance

move to sidebar hide

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Traditional Polish and Lithuanian soup made from sour, salted cucumbers and potato

|     |     |
| --- | --- |Cucumber soup
| [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Cucumber_soup.jpg/200px-Cucumber_soup.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber_soup.jpg)<br><br>_Zupa ogórkowa_ is prepared using [sour, salted cucumbers](/wiki/Polish_style_pickled_cucumber "Polish style pickled cucumber") |     |
| Alternative names | Ogórkowa |
| Course | [Soup](/wiki/Soup "Soup")<br>, [entree](/wiki/Entree "Entree") |
| Place of origin | [Poland](/wiki/Poland "Poland") |
| Region or state | [Central Europe](/wiki/Central_Europe "Central Europe") |
| Serving temperature | Hot or cold |
| Main ingredients | [Cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")<br>, [pickled cucumber](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber") |
| *   [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/16px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg)<br>     [Cookbook: Cucumber soup](https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Zupa_Og%C3%B3rkowa_(Polish_Cucumber_Soup) "b:Cookbook:Zupa Ogórkowa (Polish Cucumber Soup)")<br>    <br>*    [![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4a/Commons-logo.svg/12px-Commons-logo.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Commons-logo.svg)<br>     [Media: Cucumber soup](https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/Category:Zupa_og%C3%B3rkowa "commons:Category:Zupa ogórkowa") |     |

**Cucumber soup** is a traditional [Polish](/wiki/Polish_cuisine "Polish cuisine")
 and [Lithuanian](/wiki/Lithuanian_cuisine "Lithuanian cuisine")
 soup ([Polish](/wiki/Polish_language "Polish language")
: _Zupa ogórkowa_, [\[zupa ɔɡurkɔva\]](/wiki/Help:IPA/Polish "Help:IPA/Polish")
 [](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/3/39/Pl-zupa_og%C3%B3rkowa.ogg/Pl-zupa_og%C3%B3rkowa.ogg.mp3 "Play audio")
[ⓘ](/wiki/File:Pl-zupa_og%C3%B3rkowa.ogg "File:Pl-zupa ogórkowa.ogg")
).[\[1\]](#cite_note-Applebaum_Crittenden_Bialy_Bialy_2012-1)
 It is made from [sour, salted cucumbers](/wiki/Polish_style_pickled_cucumber "Polish style pickled cucumber")
 and [potato](/wiki/Potato "Potato")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Applebaum_Crittenden_Bialy_Bialy_2012-1)
 Occasionally, [rice](/wiki/Rice "Rice")
 is substituted for the potatoes.

Cucumber soup is also any [soup](/wiki/Soup "Soup")
 using [cucumbers](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
 as a primary ingredient, and is present in various [cuisines](/wiki/Cuisine "Cuisine")
. The two major varieties are fresh cucumber soup and pickled cucumber soup.

A similar soup is also common in [Russia](/wiki/Russian_cuisine "Russian cuisine")
 and [Ukraine](/wiki/Ukrainian_cuisine "Ukrainian cuisine")
, where it is known as _[rassolnik](/wiki/Rassolnik "Rassolnik")
_. There is another cucumber based soup known as [tarator](/wiki/Tarator "Tarator")
 in [Bulgaria](/wiki/Bulgarian_cuisine "Bulgarian cuisine")
, which is served cold.

Fresh cucumber soups
--------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_soup&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Fresh cucumber soups")\
\]

Some fresh cucumber soups are just a blend of ingredients (cucumber, spices, other vegetables or fruits, etc.) served cold, others are cooked, possibly in some kind of [broth](/wiki/Broth "Broth")
, and served either hot or chilled.

*   [![Fresh cucumber-mint soup with a yogurt dumpling](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/CucumberMintSoupYogurtDumpling_%288363451507%29.jpg/220px-CucumberMintSoupYogurtDumpling_%288363451507%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:CucumberMintSoupYogurtDumpling_(8363451507).jpg "Fresh cucumber-mint soup with a yogurt dumpling")
    
    Fresh cucumber-mint soup with a yogurt dumpling
    
*   [![Pork stuffed cucumber and zucchini soup in pork broth.](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Cucumber_and_zucchini_soup.jpg/188px-Cucumber_and_zucchini_soup.jpg)](/wiki/File:Cucumber_and_zucchini_soup.jpg "Pork stuffed cucumber and zucchini soup in pork broth.")
    
    Pork stuffed cucumber and zucchini soup in pork broth.

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

Species of flowering plant that produces cucumbers

For other uses, see [Cucumber (disambiguation)](/wiki/Cucumber_(disambiguation) "Cucumber (disambiguation)")
.

The **cucumber** (_**Cucumis sativus**_) is a widely-cultivated [creeping vine](/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants "Vine")
 plant in the family [Cucurbitaceae](/wiki/Cucurbitaceae "Cucurbitaceae")
 that bears cylindrical to spherical [fruits](/wiki/Fruit "Fruit")
, which are used as [culinary vegetables](/wiki/Culinary_vegetable "Culinary vegetable")
.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica-1)
 Considered an annual plant,[\[2\]](#cite_note-2)
 there are three main types of cucumber—slicing, [pickling](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
, and [seedless](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
—within which several [cultivars](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 have been created. The cucumber originates in [Asia](/wiki/Asia "Asia")
 extending from [India](/wiki/India "India")
, [Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal")
, [Bangladesh](/wiki/Bangladesh "Bangladesh")
, [China](/wiki/China "China")
 ([Yunnan](/wiki/Yunnan "Yunnan")
, [Guizhou](/wiki/Guizhou "Guizhou")
, [Guangxi](/wiki/Guangxi "Guangxi")
), and [Northern Thailand](/wiki/Northern_Thailand "Northern Thailand")
,[\[3\]](#cite_note-3)
[\[4\]](#cite_note-4)
[\[5\]](#cite_note-powo-5)
[\[6\]](#cite_note-6)
 but now grows on most [continents](/wiki/Continent "Continent")
, and many different types of cucumber are grown commercially and traded on the [global market](/wiki/Global_market "Global market")
. In [North America](/wiki/North_America "North America")
, the term _[wild cucumber](/wiki/Wild_cucumber "Wild cucumber")
_ refers to plants in the [genera](/wiki/Genus "Genus")
 _[Echinocystis](/wiki/Echinocystis "Echinocystis")
_ and _[Marah](/wiki/Marah_(plant) "Marah (plant)")
_, though the two are not closely related.

Description
-----------

The cucumber is a [creeping vine](/wiki/Vine#Horticultural_climbing_plants "Vine")
 that roots in the ground and grows up [trellises](/wiki/Trellis_(agriculture) "Trellis (agriculture)")
 or other supporting frames, wrapping around supports with thin, spiraling [tendrils](/wiki/Tendrils "Tendrils")
.[\[7\]](#cite_note-7)
 The plant may also root in a [soilless medium](/wiki/Soilless_gardening "Soilless gardening")
, whereby it will sprawl along the ground in lieu of a supporting structure. The vine has large leaves that form a [canopy](/wiki/Canopy_(biology) "Canopy (biology)")
 over the fruits.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

The fruit of typical cultivars of cucumber is roughly [cylindrical](/wiki/Cylinder_(geometry) "Cylinder (geometry)")
, but elongated with tapered ends, and may be as large as 62 centimeters (24 in) long and 10 centimeters (4 in) in diameter.[\[8\]](#cite_note-ZhangLi2019-8)

Cucumber fruits consist of 95% water (see nutrition table). In [botanical](/wiki/Botany "Botany")
 terms, the cucumber is classified as a [_pepo_](/wiki/Pepo_(botany) "Pepo (botany)")
, a type of [botanical berry](/wiki/Berry_(botany) "Berry (botany)")
 with a hard outer rind and no internal divisions. However, much like [tomatoes](/wiki/Tomato "Tomato")
 and [squashes](/wiki/Cucurbita "Cucurbita")
, it is often perceived, prepared, and eaten as a [vegetable](/wiki/Vegetable "Vegetable")
.[\[9\]](#cite_note-9)

### Flowering and pollination

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG/150px-Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG)](/wiki/File:Cucumis_sativus_0002.JPG)

_Cucumis sativus_ flower

|     |     |
| --- | --- |Genomic information
| [NCBI](/wiki/National_Center_for_Biotechnology_Information "National Center for Biotechnology Information")<br> genome ID | [1639](https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/genome/?term=1639) |
| [Ploidy](/wiki/Ploidy "Ploidy") | diploid |
| [Genome size](/wiki/Genome_size "Genome size") | 323.99 Mb |
| Sequenced [organelle](/wiki/Organelle "Organelle") | mitochondrion |
| Organelle size | 244.82 Mb |
| Year of completion | 2011 |

Most cucumber cultivars are seeded and require pollination. For this purpose, thousands of [honey](/wiki/Honey_bee "Honey bee")
 [beehives](/wiki/Beehive "Beehive")
 are annually carried to cucumber fields just before bloom. Cucumbers may also be pollinated via [bumblebees](/wiki/Bumblebee "Bumblebee")
 and several other bee species. Most cucumbers that require pollination are [self-incompatible](/wiki/Self_incompatibility_in_plants "Self incompatibility in plants")
, thus requiring the [pollen](/wiki/Pollen "Pollen")
 of another plant in order to form [seeds](/wiki/Seed "Seed")
 and fruit.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)
 Some self-compatible cultivars exist that are related to the 'Lemon cucumber' cultivar.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)

A few [cultivars](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 of cucumber are [parthenocarpic](/wiki/Parthenocarpy "Parthenocarpy")
, the [blossoms](/wiki/Blossom "Blossom")
 of which create [seedless fruit](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
 without [pollination](/wiki/Pollination "Pollination")
, which degrades the eating quality of these cultivar. In the [United States](/wiki/Agriculture_in_the_United_States "Agriculture in the United States")
, these are usually grown in [greenhouses](/wiki/Greenhouse "Greenhouse")
, where [bees](/wiki/Bee "Bee")
 are excluded. In [Europe](/wiki/Europe "Europe")
, they are grown outdoors in some regions, where bees are likewise excluded.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

Traditional cultivars produce male blossoms first, then female, in about equivalent numbers. Newer [gynoecious](/wiki/Gynoecious "Gynoecious")
 hybrid cultivars produce almost all female blossoms. They may have a [pollenizer](/wiki/Pollenizer "Pollenizer")
 cultivar interplanted, and the number of beehives per unit area is increased, but temperature changes induce male flowers even on these plants, which may be sufficient for pollination to occur.[\[10\]](#cite_note-Nonnecke-10)

In 2009, an international team of researchers announced they had sequenced the cucumber [genome](/wiki/Genome "Genome")
.[\[11\]](#cite_note-11)

A study of [genetic recombination](/wiki/Genetic_recombination "Genetic recombination")
 during [meiosis](/wiki/Meiosis "Meiosis")
 in cucumber provided a high resolution landscape of meiotic [DNA double strand-breaks](/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring) "DNA damage (naturally occurring)")
 and [genetic crossovers](/wiki/Chromosomal_crossover "Chromosomal crossover")
.[\[12\]](#cite_note-Wang2023-12)
 The average number of crossovers per chromosome per meiosis was 0.92 to 0.99.[\[12\]](#cite_note-Wang2023-12)

### Herbivore defense

[Phytochemicals](/wiki/Phytochemical "Phytochemical")
 in cucumbers may discourage natural [foraging](/wiki/Foraging "Foraging")
 by [herbivores](/wiki/Herbivore "Herbivore")
, such as insects, [nematodes](/wiki/Nematode "Nematode")
 or [wildlife](/wiki/Wildlife "Wildlife")
.[\[13\]](#cite_note-shang-13)
 As a possible defense mechanism, cucumbers produce [cucurbitacin C](/wiki/Cucurbitacin "Cucurbitacin")
,[\[14\]](#cite_note-:0a-14)
 which causes a [bitter taste](/wiki/Bitter_(taste) "Bitter (taste)")
 in some cucumber varieties. This potential mechanism is under preliminary research to identify whether cucumbers are able to deter herbivores and [environmental stresses](/wiki/Biotic_stress#Effect_on_plant_growth "Biotic stress")
 by using an intrinsic [chemical defense](/wiki/Chemical_defense#Plants "Chemical defense")
, particularly in the leaves, [cotyledons](/wiki/Cotyledon "Cotyledon")
, [pedicel](/wiki/Pedicel_(botany) "Pedicel (botany)")
, [carpopodium](/wiki/Caropodium "Caropodium")
, and fruit.[\[14\]](#cite_note-:0a-14)
[\[15\]](#cite_note-15)

| †Percentages estimated using [US recommendations](/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values "Reference Daily Intake")<br> for adults,[\[16\]](#cite_note-FDADailyValues-16)<br> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from [the National Academies](/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine "National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine")<br>.[\[17\]](#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-17) |     |

Raw cucumber (with [peel](/wiki/Peel_(fruit) "Peel (fruit)")
) is 95% water, 4% [carbohydrates](/wiki/Carbohydrate "Carbohydrate")
, 1% [protein](/wiki/Protein "Protein")
, and contains negligible [fat](/wiki/Fat "Fat")
. A 100-gram (3+1⁄2\-ounce) [reference serving](/wiki/Serving_size "Serving size")
 provides 65 kilojoules (16 kilocalories) of [food energy](/wiki/Food_energy "Food energy")
. It has a low content of [micronutrients](/wiki/Micronutrient "Micronutrient")
: it is notable only for [vitamin K](/wiki/Vitamin_K "Vitamin K")
, at 14% of the [Daily Value](/wiki/Daily_Value "Daily Value")
 (table).

Depending on variety, cucumbers may have a mild [melon](/wiki/Melon "Melon")
 aroma and flavor, in part resulting from unsaturated [aldehydes](/wiki/Aldehyde "Aldehyde")
, such as [(E,Z)-nona-2,6-dienal](/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal "Trans,cis-2,6-nonadienal")
, and the [_cis_\- and _trans_\-](/wiki/Cis%E2%80%93trans_isomerism "Cis–trans isomerism")
 [isomers](/wiki/Isomer "Isomer")
 of [2-nonenal](/wiki/2-Nonenal "2-Nonenal")
.[\[18\]](#cite_note-18)
 The slightly [bitter](/wiki/Bitter_(taste) "Bitter (taste)")
 taste of cucumber rind results from [cucurbitacins](/wiki/Cucurbitacins "Cucurbitacins")
.[\[19\]](#cite_note-19)

Varieties
---------

See also: [List of cucumber varieties](/wiki/List_of_cucumber_varieties "List of cucumber varieties")

In general [cultivation](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture")
, cucumbers are classified into three main [cultivar](/wiki/Cultivar "Cultivar")
 groups: slicing, [pickled](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
, and [seedless/burpless](/wiki/Seedless_fruit "Seedless fruit")
.

Uses
----

### Culinary

#### Slicing

Cucumbers grown to eat fresh are called _slicing cucumbers_. The main varieties of slicers mature on [vines](/wiki/Vine "Vine")
 with large leaves that provide shading.[\[20\]](#cite_note-20)

Slicers grown commercially for the North American market are generally longer, smoother, more uniform in color, and have much tougher skin. In contrast, those in other countries, often called [European cucumbers](/wiki/European_cucumber "European cucumber")
, are smaller and have thinner, more delicate skin, often with fewer seeds, thus are often sold in plastic skin for protection. This variety may also be called a _telegraph cucumber_, particularly in [Australasia](/wiki/Australasia "Australasia")
.[\[21\]](#cite_note-21)

#### Pickling

Main article: [Pickled cucumber](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")

[Pickling](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber "Pickled cucumber")
 with [brine](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Brined_pickles "Pickled cucumber")
, sugar, [vinegar](/wiki/Vinegar "Vinegar")
, and spices creates various flavored products from cucumbers and other foods.[\[22\]](#cite_note-avi-22)
 Although any cucumber can be pickled, commercial pickles are made from cucumbers specially bred for uniformity of length-to-diameter ratio and lack of voids in the flesh. Those cucumbers intended for pickling, called _picklers_, grow to about 7 to 10 cm (3 to 4 in) long and 2.5 cm (1 in) wide. Compared to slicers, picklers tend to be shorter, thicker, less-regularly shaped, and have bumpy skin with tiny white or black-dotted spines. Color can vary from creamy yellow to pale or dark green.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

#### Gherkin

[Gherkins](/wiki/Pickled_cucumber#Gherkin "Pickled cucumber")
, also called _cornichons_,[\[23\]](#cite_note-kitchn-23)
 or _baby pickles_, are small cucumbers, typically those 2.5 to 12.5 centimetres (1 to 5 in) in length, often with bumpy skin, which are typically used for pickling.[\[24\]](#cite_note-zon-24)
[\[25\]](#cite_note-wifss-25)
[\[26\]](#cite_note-india-26)
 The word _gherkin_ comes from the early modern [Dutch](/wiki/Dutch_language "Dutch language")
 _gurken_ or _augurken_ ('small pickled cucumber').[\[27\]](#cite_note-27)
 The term is also used in the name for _[Cucumis anguria](/wiki/Cucumis_anguria "Cucumis anguria")
_, the _West Indian gherkin_, a closely related species.[\[28\]](#cite_note-28)

#### Burpless

Burpless cucumbers are sweeter and have a thinner skin than other varieties of cucumber. They are reputed to be easy to digest and to have a pleasant taste. They can grow as long as 60 centimeters (2 ft), are nearly seedless, and have a delicate skin. Most commonly grown in greenhouses, these [parthenocarpic](/wiki/Parthenocarpic "Parthenocarpic")
 cucumbers are often found in [grocery markets](/wiki/Grocery_store "Grocery store")
, [shrink-wrapped](/wiki/Shrink_wrap "Shrink wrap")
 in plastic. They are marketed as either burpless or seedless, as the seeds and skin of other varieties of cucumbers are said to give some people gas.[\[29\]](#cite_note-29)

----------

In 2022, world production of cucumbers and gherkins was 95 million [tonnes](/wiki/Tonne "Tonne")
, led by China with 82% of the total.[\[30\]](#cite_note-faostat-30)

Cultivation history
-------------------

Cultivated for at least 3,000 years, the cultivated cucumbers _"Cucumis sativus"_ were domesticated in [India](/wiki/India "India")
 from wild "_C. sativus var. hardwickii_".[\[31\]](#cite_note-31)
[\[32\]](#cite_note-32)
[\[33\]](#cite_note-33)
 where a great many varieties have been observed, along with its closest living relative, _[Cucumis hystrix](/wiki/Cucumis_hystrix "Cucumis hystrix")
_.[\[34\]](#cite_note-34)
 Three main cultivar groups of cucumber are namely Eurasian cucumbers (slicing cucumbers eaten raw and immature), East Asian cucumbers (pickling cucumbers) and Xishuangbanna cucumbers. Based on demographic modelling, the East Asian C. sativus cultivars diverged from the Indian cultivars c. 2500 years ago.[\[35\]](#cite_note-35)
 It was probably introduced to Europe by the [Greeks](/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Greece "Agriculture in ancient Greece")
 or [Romans](/wiki/Agriculture_in_ancient_Rome "Agriculture in ancient Rome")
. Records of cucumber cultivation appear in [France](/wiki/France "France")
 in the 9th century, [England](/wiki/Agriculture_in_England "Agriculture in England")
 in the 14th century, and in North America by the mid-16th century.[\[1\]](#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica-1)
[\[36\]](#cite_note-Renner_2007-36)
[\[37\]](#cite_note-Doijode-37)
[\[38\]](#cite_note-38)

### Roman Empire

According to [Pliny the Elder](/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder "Pliny the Elder")
, the Emperor [Tiberius](/wiki/Tiberius "Tiberius")
 had the cucumber on his table daily during summer and winter. In order to have it available for his table every day of the year, the Romans reportedly used artificial methods of growing (similar to the [greenhouse system](/wiki/Greenhouse "Greenhouse")
), whereby _mirrorstone_ refers to Pliny's _lapis specularis_, believed to have been sheet [mica](/wiki/Mica "Mica")
:[\[39\]](#cite_note-AncientInventions-39)
[\[40\]](#cite_note-40)

> Indeed, he was never without it; for he had raised beds made in frames upon wheels, by means of which the cucumbers were moved and exposed to the full heat of the sun; while, in winter, they were withdrawn, and placed under the protection of frames glazed with mirrorstone.
> 
> — Pliny the Elder, _[Natural History](/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny) "Natural History (Pliny)")
> _ XIX.xxiii, "Vegetables of a Cartilaginous Nature—Cucumbers. Pepones"

Reportedly, they were also cultivated in _specularia_, cucumber houses glazed with oiled cloth.[\[39\]](#cite_note-AncientInventions-39)
 Pliny describes the Italian fruit as very small, probably like a [gherkin](/wiki/Gherkin "Gherkin")
. He also describes the preparation of a medication known as _elaterium_. However, some scholars\[_[who?](/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions "Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch")\
_\] believe that he was instead referring to _[Ecballium elaterium](/wiki/Ecballium_elaterium "Ecballium elaterium")
_, known in pre-[Linnean](/wiki/Linnean_nomenclature "Linnean nomenclature")
 times as _Cucumis silvestris_ or _Cucumis asininus_ ('wild cucumber' or 'donkey cucumber'), a species different from the common cucumber.[\[41\]](#cite_note-41)
 Pliny also writes about several other varieties of cucumber, including the cultivated cucumber,[\[42\]](#cite_note-42)
 and remedies from the different types (9 from the cultivated; 5 from the "anguine;" and 26 from the "wild").

### Middle Ages

[Charlemagne](/wiki/Charlemagne "Charlemagne")
 had cucumbers grown in his gardens in the 8th/9th century. They were reportedly introduced into England in the early 14th century, lost, then reintroduced approximately 250 years later. The [Spaniards](/wiki/Spaniards "Spaniards")
 (through the [Italian](/wiki/Italian_people "Italian people")
 [Christopher Columbus](/wiki/Christopher_Columbus "Christopher Columbus")
) brought cucumbers to [Haiti](/wiki/Haiti "Haiti")
 in 1494. In 1535, [Jacques Cartier](/wiki/Jacques_Cartier "Jacques Cartier")
, a French explorer, found "very great cucumbers" grown on the site of what is now [Montreal](/wiki/Montreal "Montreal")
.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

### Early-modern age

[![trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal, or cucumber aldehyde](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png/250px-Trans-2%2Ccis-6-Nonadienal.png)](/wiki/File:Trans-2,cis-6-Nonadienal.png)

[Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal](/wiki/Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal "Trans,cis-2,6-Nonadienal")
, or _cucumber aldehyde_, is a component of the distinctive aroma of cucumbers.

Throughout the 16th century, European trappers, traders, [bison](/wiki/Bison "Bison")
 hunters, and explorers bartered for the products of American Indian [agriculture](/wiki/Agriculture "Agriculture")
. The tribes of the [Great Plains](/wiki/Great_Plains "Great Plains")
 and the [Rocky Mountains](/wiki/Rocky_Mountains "Rocky Mountains")
 learned from the Spanish how to grow European crops. The farmers on the Great Plains included the [Mandan](/wiki/Mandan "Mandan")
 and [Abenaki](/wiki/Abenaki "Abenaki")
. They obtained cucumbers and [watermelons](/wiki/Watermelon "Watermelon")
 from the Spanish, and added them to the crops they were already growing, including several varieties of [corn](/wiki/Maize "Maize")
 and [beans](/wiki/Bean "Bean")
, [pumpkins](/wiki/Pumpkin "Pumpkin")
, [squash](/wiki/Squash_(fruit) "Squash (fruit)")
, and [gourd](/wiki/Gourd "Gourd")
 plants.[\[43\]](#cite_note-43)
 The [Iroquois](/wiki/Iroquois "Iroquois")
 were also growing them when the first Europeans visited them.[\[44\]](#cite_note-44)

In 1630, the Reverend [Francis Higginson](/wiki/Francis_Higginson "Francis Higginson")
 produced a book called _New-Englands Plantation_ in which, describing a garden on Conant's Island in [Boston Harbor](/wiki/Boston_Harbor "Boston Harbor")
 known as _The Governor's Garden_, he states:[\[45\]](#cite_note-45)

> The countrie aboundeth naturally with store of roots of great varietie \[_[sic](/wiki/Sic "Sic")\
> _\] and good to eat. Our turnips, parsnips, and carrots are here both bigger and sweeter than is ordinary to be found in England. Here are store of pompions, cowcumbers, and other things of that nature which I know not...

In _New England Prospect_ (1633, England), William Wood published observations he made in 1629 in America:[\[46\]](#cite_note-46)

> The ground affords very good kitchin gardens, for Turneps, Parsnips, Carrots, Radishes, and Pompions, Muskmillons, Isquoter-squashes, coucumbars, Onyons, and whatever grows well in England grows as well there, many things being better and larger. \[_[sic](/wiki/Sic "Sic")\
> _\]

### Age of Enlightenment and later

[![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg/220px-Lobster%2C_Crab%2C_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg)](/wiki/File:Lobster,_Crab,_and_a_Cucumber_-_1891P32.jpg)

_Lobster, Crab, and a Cucumber_ by [William Henry Hunt](/wiki/William_Henry_Hunt_(painter) "William Henry Hunt (painter)")
 (watercolour, 1826 or 1827)

In the later 17th century, a prejudice developed against uncooked vegetables and fruits. A number of articles in contemporary health publications stated that uncooked plants brought on summer diseases and should be forbidden to children. The cucumber kept this reputation for an inordinate period of time, "fit only for consumption by cows," which some believe is why it gained the name, _cowcumber_.\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

[Samuel Pepys](/wiki/Samuel_Pepys "Samuel Pepys")
 wrote in his diary on 22 August 1663:[\[47\]](#cite_note-47)

> \[T\]his day Sir W. Batten tells me that Mr. Newburne is dead of eating cowcumbers, of which the other day I heard of another, I think.

John Evelyn in 1699 wrote that the cucumber, 'however dress'd, was thought fit to be thrown away, being accounted little better than poyson (poison)'.[\[48\]](#cite_note-48)
[\[49\]](#cite_note-49)

According to 18th-century British writer [Samuel Johnson](/wiki/Samuel_Johnson "Samuel Johnson")
, it was commonly said among English physicians that a cucumber "should be well sliced, and dressed with pepper and vinegar, and then thrown out, as good for nothing."[\[50\]](#cite_note-50)

A copper [etching](/wiki/Etching "Etching")
 made by Maddalena Bouchard between 1772 and 1793 shows this plant to have smaller, almost bean-shaped fruits, and small yellow flowers. The small form of the cucumber is figured in [Herbals](/wiki/Herbal "Herbal")
 of the 16th century, however stating that "\[i\]f hung in a tube while in blossom, the Cucumber will grow to a most surprising length."\[_[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")\
_\]

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

![](https://en.wikipedia.org/upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg/40px-Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg.png)

Look up _**[cucumber](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cucumber "wiktionary:cucumber")
**_, _**[Cucumis sativus](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Cucumis_sativus "wiktionary:Cucumis sativus")
**_, or _**[cuke](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cuke "wiktionary:cuke")
**_ in Wiktionary, the free dictionary.

A **[cucumber](/wiki/Cucumber "Cucumber")
** is an edible vegetable.

**Cucumber** may also refer to:

Arts and entertainment
----------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=1 "Edit section: Arts and entertainment")\
\]

*   [_Cucumber_ (British TV series)](/wiki/Cucumber_(British_TV_series) "Cucumber (British TV series)")
    , a 2015 British television series
*   [Cucumber (card game)](/wiki/Cucumber_(card_game) "Cucumber (card game)")
    , a north European card game of Swedish origin
*   [_Cucumber_ (Canadian TV series)](/wiki/Cucumber_(Canadian_TV_series) "Cucumber (Canadian TV series)")
    , a Canadian children's television series originally broadcast in the 1970s
*   [The Cucumbers](/wiki/The_Cucumbers "The Cucumbers")
    , a 1980s power pop/new wave band from New Jersey

Plants
------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Plants")\
\]

*   [Armenian cucumber](/wiki/Armenian_cucumber "Armenian cucumber")
    , an edible vegetable closely related to the common cucumber
*   [Cucumber tree](/wiki/Cucumber_tree "Cucumber tree")
    , several unrelated trees
*   [Wild cucumber (disambiguation)](/wiki/Wild_cucumber_(disambiguation) "Wild cucumber (disambiguation)")
    , several species of plants similar to cucumbers

Other
-----

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Cucumber_(disambiguation)&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Other")\
\]

*   [Cucumber (software)](/wiki/Cucumber_(software) "Cucumber (software)")
    , a behavior-driven development tool
*   [Cucumber, West Virginia](/wiki/Cucumber,_West_Virginia "Cucumber, West Virginia")
    , a community in West Virginia
*   [Cucumber beetle](/wiki/Cucumber_beetle "Cucumber beetle")
    , a type of beetle
*   [Sea cucumber](/wiki/Sea_cucumber "Sea cucumber")
     (_Holothuroidea_), a type of animal
*   "[Cucumber King](/wiki/Cucumber_King "Cucumber King")
    " (Nyaung-u Sawrahan), a ruler of the kingdom of Pagan in what is now Burma
*   The top level on the 1 to 4 EHS scale developed by the [European Association of Urology](/wiki/European_Association_of_Urology "European Association of Urology")
